        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1331023164">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14891132577242520376"><strong>4.5 Stars</strong><br /><br /><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1444831116i/16560337.jpg" /><br /><br />I asked a fellow blogger and fan of To Kill a Mocking Bird....<em> "How does one review this book without writing a complete thesis on it?"</em> The simple answer is, it's nigh on impossible. There's no short or succinct way to fully express the intricacies weaved through the layers that are showcased in this coming of age story. Layers that eloquently depict the hypocrisies and injustices of life during its historical time-frame. The story explores courage, convictions, hope, race, class, preju</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14891132577242520376"><strong>4.5 Stars</strong><br /><br /><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1444831116i/16560337.jpg" /><br /><br />I asked a fellow blogger and fan of To Kill a Mocking Bird....<em> "How does one review this book without writing a complete thesis on it?"</em> The simple answer is, it's nigh on impossible. There's no short or succinct way to fully express the intricacies weaved through the layers that are showcased in this coming of age story. Layers that eloquently depict the hypocrisies and injustices of life during its historical time-frame. The story explores courage, convictions, hope, race, class, prejudice, acceptance and equality. It's steeped in the history of 1930's life in deep South, America and is told through the eyes of eight year old Scout Finch who's surrounded by interesting characters and situations crafted together to produce thought provoking nuances and scenarios worthy of analysis and praise. <br /><br /><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1444831116i/16560338.jpg" /><br /><br />To Kill a Mocking bird is a book I've longed to read for some time. Most readers I've discussed the story with have long since read or studied it. (I'm fairly late to this book party!) Most have a deep affection for the story and I can truly understand why, the prose is certainly capable of providing a thought provoking and stimulating discussion. In all honesty I struggled to connect with the story for around the first 32% which I now see as Harper Lee luring me into her fictional world, ensnaring me with her characters, visually vivid descriptions and her simply told, yet poignant story packed with complexity and deep meaning. At 32% I felt myself turn into the story and I practically devoured the remaining book during the course of 24 hours, eager to learn of Tom Robinson's fate and the consequences for Atticus and his family.<br /><br /><img width="400" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1444831116i/16560339.jpg" /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14891132577242520376">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

A retired English professor who began writing fiction at the age of 70, Maclean produced what is now re&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText8925038477106508576\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;Just as Norman Maclean writes at the end of &amp;quot;A River Runs through It&amp;quot; that he is &amp;quot;haunted by waters,&amp;quot; so have readers been haunted by his novella. A retired English professor who began writing fiction at the age of 70, Maclean produced what is now recognized as one of the classic American stories of the twentieth century. Originally published in 1976, A River Runs through It and Other Stories now celebrates its twenty-fifth anniversary, marked by this new edition that includes a foreword by Annie Proulx.\n\nMaclean grew up in the western Rocky Mountains in the first decades of the twentieth century. As a young man he worked many summers in logging camps and for the United States Forest Service. The two novellas and short story in this collection are based on his own experiences—the experiences of a young man who found that life was only a step from art in its structures and beauty. The beauty he found was in reality, and so he leaves a careful record of what it was like to work in the woods when it was still a world of horse and hand and foot, without power saws, &amp;quot;cats,&amp;quot; or four-wheel drives. Populated with drunks, loggers, card sharks, and whores, and set in the small towns and surrounding trout streams and mountains of western Montana, the stories concern themselves with the complexities of fly fishing, logging, fighting forest fires, playing cribbage, and being a husband, a son, and a father.\n\nBy turns raunchy, poignant, caustic, and elegiac, these are superb tales which express, in Maclean\'s own words, &amp;quot;a little of the love I have for the earth as it goes by.&amp;quot; A first offering from a 70-year-old writer, the basis of a top-grossing movie, and the first original fiction published by the University of Chicago Press, A River Runs through It and Other Stories has sold more than a million copies. As Proulx writes in her foreword to this new edition, &amp;quot;In 1990 Norman Maclean died in body, but for hundreds of thousands of readers he will live as long as fish swim and books are made.&amp;quot;\n\n&amp;quot;Altogether beautiful in the power of its feeling... As beautiful as anything in Thoreau or Hemingway.&amp;quot; —Alfred Kazin, Chicago Tribune Book World\n\n&amp;quot;It is an enchanted tale... I have read the story three times now, and each time it seems fuller.&amp;quot; —Roger Sale, New York Review of Books\n\n&amp;quot;Maclean\'s book—acerbic, laconic, deadpan—rings out of a rich American tradition that includes Mark Twain, Kin Hubbard, Richard Bissell, Jean Shepherd, and Nelson Algren. I love its sound.&amp;quot; —James R. Frakes, New York Times Book Review\n\n&amp;quot;The title novella is the prize... Something unique and marvelous: a story that is at once an evocation of nature\'s miracles and realities and a probing of human mysteries. Wise, witty, wonderful, Maclean spins his tales, casts his flies, fishes the rivers and the woods for what he remembers from his youth in the Rockies.&amp;quot; —Publishers Weekly\n\n&amp;quot;Ostensibly a \'fishing story,\' \'A River Runs through It\' is really an autobiographical elegy that captivates readers who have never held a fly rod in their hand. In it the art of casting a fly becomes a ritual of grace, a metaphor for man\'s attempt to move into nature.&amp;quot; —Andrew Rosenheim, The Independent&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a The protagonist of Booth Tarkington\'s great historical drama is George Amberson&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText6833330784994447954\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;Winner of the Pulitzer Prize when it was first published in 1918, The Magnificent Ambersons chronicles the changing fortunes of three generations of an American dynasty. The protagonist of Booth Tarkington\'s great historical drama is George Amberson Minafer, the spoiled and arrogant grandson of the founder of the family\'s magnificence. Eclipsed by a new breed of developers, financiers, and manufacturers, this pampered scion begins his gradual descent from the midwestern aristocracy to the working class. Today The Magnificent Ambersons is best known through the 1942 Orson Welles movie, but as the critic Stanley Kauffmann noted, &amp;quot;It is high time that [the novel] appear again, to stand outside the force of Welles\'s genius, confident in its own right.&amp;quot; &amp;quot;The Magnificent Ambersons is perhaps Tarkington\'s best novel,&amp;quot; judged Van Wyck Brooks. &amp;quot;[It is] a typical story of an American family and town--the great family that locally ruled the roost and vanished virtually in a day as the town spread and darkened into a city. This novel no doubt was a permanent page in the social history of the United States, so admirably conceived and written was the tale of the Amber-sons, their house, their fate and the growth of the community in which they were submerged in the end.&amp;quot;\n\nBooth Tarkington (1869-1946), a prolific writer who achieved overnight success with his first novel, The Gentleman from Indiana (1899), is perhaps best remembered as the author of the popular Penrod adventures and Seventeen (1916).     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1825.Harper_Lee">About Harper Lee</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/1825.Harper_Lee"><img alt="1825" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1188820730p2/1825.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer11481998577946980121">Harper Lee, known as Nelle, was born in the Alabama town of Monroeville, the youngest of four children of Amasa Coleman Lee and Frances Cunningham Finch Lee. Her father, a former newspaper editor and proprietor, was a lawyer who served on the state legislature from 1926 to 1938. As a child, Lee was a tomboy and a precocious reader, and enjoyed the friendship of her schoolmate and neighbor, the you</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11481998577946980121">Harper Lee, known as Nelle, was born in the Alabama town of Monroeville, the youngest of four children of Amasa Coleman Lee and Frances Cunningham Finch Lee. Her father, a former newspaper editor and proprietor, was a lawyer who served on the state legislature from 1926 to 1938. As a child, Lee was a tomboy and a precocious reader, and enjoyed the friendship of her schoolmate and neighbor, the young Truman Capote.<br /><br />After graduating from high school in Monroeville, Lee enrolled at the all-female Huntingdon College in Montgomery (1944-45), and then pursued a law degree at the University of Alabama (1945-50), pledging the Chi Omega sorority. While there, she wrote for several student publications and spent a year as editor of the campus humor magazine, "Ramma-Jamma". Though she did not complete the law degree, she studied for a summer in Oxford, England, before moving to New York in 1950, where she worked as a reservation clerk with Eastern Air Lines and BOAC.<br /><br />Lee continued as a reservation clerk until the late 50s, when she devoted herself to writing. She lived a frugal life, traveling between her cold-water-only apartment in New York to her family home in Alabama to care for her father.<br /><br />Having written several long stories, Harper Lee located an agent in November 1956. The following month at the East 50th townhouse of her friends Michael Brown and Joy Williams Brown, she received a gift of a year's wages with a note: "You have one year off from your job to write whatever you please. Merry Christmas." <br /><br />Within a year, she had a first draft. Working with J. B. Lippincott &amp; Co. editor Tay Hohoff, she completed <em>To Kill a Mockingbird</em> in the summer of 1959. Published July 11, 1960, the novel was an immediate bestseller and won great critical acclaim, including the Pulitzer Prize for Fiction in 1961. It remains a bestseller with more than 30 million copies in print. In 1999, it was voted "Best Novel of the Century" in a poll by the Library Journal.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11481998577946980121">...more</a>

    </div>

Twenty-six-year-old Jean Louise Finch--\"Scout\"--returns home from New York City to visit her agi&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;span id=\"freeText5374452598931028442\" style=\"display:none\"&gt;From Harper Lee comes a landmark new novel set two decades after her beloved Pulitzer Prize-winning masterpiece, To Kill a Mockingbird.\n\nMaycomb, Alabama. Twenty-six-year-old Jean Louise Finch--&amp;quot;Scout&amp;quot;--returns home from New York City to visit her aging father, Atticus. Set against the backdrop of the civil rights tensions and political turmoil that were transforming the South, Jean Louise\'s homecoming turns bittersweet when she learns disturbing truths about her close-knit family, the town and the people dearest to her. Memories from her childhood flood back, and her values and assumptions are thrown into doubt. Featuring many of the iconic characters from To Kill a Mockingbird, Go Set a Watchman perfectly captures a young woman, and a world, in a painful yet necessary transition out of the illusions of the past--a journey that can be guided only by one\'s conscience. \n\nWritten in the mid-1950s, Go Set a Watchman imparts a fuller, richer understanding and appreciation of Harper Lee. Here is an unforgettable novel of wisdom, humanity, passion, humor and effortless precision--a profoundly affecting work of art that is both wonderfully evocative of another era and relevant to our own times. It not only confirms the enduring brilliance of To Kill a Mockingbird, but also serves as its essential companion, adding depth, context and new meaning to an American classic.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a
